    <div class="section">
      

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review nosyndicate" id="review_4133566">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/4133566" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/26852-eric" class="left imgcol" title="Eric"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1383287017p2/26852.jpg" alt="Eric" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/4133566?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">May 21, 2010</a>

      <a href="/user/show/26852-eric" class="user" title="Eric">Eric</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/208779.Mansfield_Park" title="Paperback 9780140434149" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/26852-eric?shelf=unexpected" class="actionLinkLite">unexpected</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer4133566">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer925820328358962598">My reading of <em>Mansfield Park</em> was attended, part of the way, by two poets talking about the difficulty of writing (or to me, reading) Austen’s kind of novel:<br /><br /><br /><blockquote>A young poet’s ignorance of life will go unnoticed. Meter, rhyme, felicitous phrases, and what not mask the underlying weakness or banality. With fiction, where dissimilar characters suffer and grow and interact, there is no place to hide. One either knows what people go through or doesn’t.</blockquote> (James Merrill)<br /><br /><br /><blockquote>Then she’s a novelist. I don’t know w</blockquote></span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText925820328358962598">My reading of <em>Mansfield Park</em> was attended, part of the way, by two poets talking about the difficulty of writing (or to me, reading) Austen’s kind of novel:<br /><br /><br /><blockquote>A young poet’s ignorance of life will go unnoticed. Meter, rhyme, felicitous phrases, and what not mask the underlying weakness or banality. With fiction, where dissimilar characters suffer and grow and interact, there is no place to hide. One either knows what people go through or doesn’t.</blockquote> (James Merrill)<br /><br /><br /><blockquote>Then she’s a novelist. I don’t know whether <br />You will agree, but novel writing is<br />A higher art than poetry altogether<br />In my opinion, and success implies<br />Both finer character and faculties.<br /><br />[…:]<br /><br />The average poet in comparison,<br />Is unobservant, immature, and lazy.<br />You must admit, when all is said and done,<br />His sense of Other People’s very hazy…</blockquote> (W.H. Auden)<br /><br /><br />I don’t quite agree with Auden, but it is right that in his “New Year Letter” Austen should stand for the novel at its most dramatically characterful, social-minded and prosy. Austen knows what people go through together. Her dissimilar characters grow and suffer and interact credibly—and she pursues the meaning of their chambered clashes with as little concern for “felicitous phrases, and what not” as any novelist I’ve ever read. Austen’s prose isn’t poetically nerved, it doesn’t aspire to “the condition of music,” and oftentimes it resembles nothing so much as a fine-scratching needle registering the sleights, snubs, anxieties and egotistic vibrations of drawing room seismology. She is no poet, C. Brontë complained to G.H. Lewes. For these reasons—or for dim undergrad apprehension of such—she strongly repelled me when first assigned. <br /><br /><br />Encounters with <em>Pride and Prejudice</em> in college had me fleeing what I saw, then, as the parched and juiceless tabulation of the small change of existence. Claustral, airless, over-analytic of things I didn’t care about. Had I known, I would have seconded Emerson’s, de Staël’s judgment of Austen as “vulgar”/ “vulgaire.” You must understand that to a certain type of youth, the understated is unreal, the commonplace contemptible, the socially conditioned self a soulless puppet. Marriage—marriage as mundane proximity, not Shelleyan epipsyche—is personally remote, at the same time that it is made disgustingly familiar by the misalliance of one’s parents, all parents; and the comedies of courtship and fortune seem trivial when shelved next to whaling and war and revolution and dark journeys and <em>guignon</em> and the horror, the horror! The prose I was bred to is fantastic, fortissimo, and monologic. Books were an escape from the idiocy of family life, not the means of its exploration. My sense of other people was hazy and I liked it that way. The friction of confined egos, however wisely told, reminded me of home; and Austen’s domesticities were closed to me. I wanted only the unreliably (but poetically) narrated novel, the dialogue-less recitation; unpeopled reveries, lyric tirades; <em>Notes from Underground</em>, <em>Sartor Resartus</em>, <em>Thus Spoke Zarathustra</em>. <br /><br /><br />If Austen could scrutinize readerships since 1817, she’d surely have sharp things to say about the current crop of Catherine Morelands, with their Dracula Beefcake books; but I think she’d be equally amused, in her prosy Augustan way, by the American sons of Carlyle, the French sons of Poe, the Nietzscheans, the Lawrentians, the Beats—by all the poseurs of extreme consciousness bred by a Romanticism she knew only in its initial generation and Gothic foretaste. The days of my exclusive allegiance to this readership are long gone, but I only this year made time for its traditional <em>bête noire</em>. Why <em>Mansfield Park</em>? Lionel Trilling, his brow lined and eyes darkened by office hours spent brooding on the moral crises of modernity, announces, after a meditative drag on his Chesterfield, that “there is scarcely one of our modern pieties that it does not offend.” Oh, sign me up! And the old Ishmaelite prejudice may be at work, as well: the Austen novel people say is so unlike the rest is the Austen novel I want to read. <br /><br /><br /><br /><strong>Lady Bertram</strong><br /><br /><em>Every thing that a considerate parent</em> ought <em>to feel was advanced for her use; and every thing that an affectionate mother</em> must <em>feel in promoting her children’s enjoyment, was attributed to her nature.</em><br /><br />Is there a better description of the ghostly, marginal parent whose presence in the family circle amounts to a flickering, semi-transparent hologram beamed from the hopeful head of the engaged parent? In this novel’s gallery of hazily inattentive and self-absorbed people, Lady Bertram stands out. <br /><br /><br /><br /><strong>Mrs. Norris</strong><br /><br />That you always know <em>what</em> Mrs. Norris will say, but never <em>how</em> she will say it, is one sign of Austen’s dramatic genius. Mrs. Norris has a predictable store of topics, but the surprise is the ingenuity with which she insinuates her resentful bullying, her aggressive self-pity, into <em>any</em> matter under discussion. This is absolutely realistic. There are narcissists, dead to the world and to other people, who impart an illusion of vibrancy by the sheer variety of excuses they find to talk about their one or two emotions. <br /><br /><br /><br /><strong>Mary Crawford</strong><br /><br />One of my favorite characters. A showcase of ambiguity. Mary is where wit borders vapidity, where charm blends with oafishness. <br /><br /><blockquote>Of Mary Crawford, whose charm almost equals her brother’s, we are led to expect that her vivaciousness and audacity will constitute a beneficent counter-principle to the stodginess which, as the novel freely grants, is one of the main attributes of Mansfield Park. But in the outcome her wit is seen to be by no means an energy of Spirit pressing forward to new and freer and more developed modes of being. Actually its tendency is regressive—its depreciation of Mansfield Park is not an effort of liberation but an acquiescence in bondage, a cynical commitment to the way of the world...</blockquote><br /><br />Trilling does not go on to illustrate his point with reference to the discussion of the clergy Edmund, Mary and Fanny conduct over chapters 9, 10, and 11, but I think he must have had it in mind. This running discussion is a bravura set-piece, and the aha! of revelation-through-dialogue is Mary’s cloddish inability—her smug refusal—to square handsome Edmund’s imminent ordination with the yawning parishioners, homely parsons and indolent clergymen who populate her handbook of received ideas. Mary’s refusal to credit the existence of a figure present before her eyes but not present in the fashionable gallery of burlesques, her slavish reliance on chic formulae of wit, make her, ironically, unworldly, provincial, and stodgy. Edmund and Fanny, the novel’s nominal prigs, actually have livelier, more sophisticated minds: like Mary they are aware of the ignobility of some clergy, while remaining receptive to the nobility of the office and the possible dignity of its holders; they know clergymen to be gluttonous placeholders, and they know them to be “the masters of the ceremonies of life”—a complex vision, a dual perception of reality and of the ideality possible within it that Ruskin saw as instinct with the highest consciousness.<br /><br /><br />Another instance of neurasthenic Fanny’s paradoxical liveliness is her attitude during the tour of Sotherton:<br /><br /><blockquote>The whole party rose accordingly, and under Mrs. Rushworth's guidance were shewn through a number of rooms, all lofty, and many large, and amply furnished in the taste of fifty years back, with shining floors, solid mahogany, rich damask, marble, gilding and carving, each handsome in its way. Of pictures there were abundance, and some few good, but the larger part were family portraits, no longer any thing to any body but Mrs. Rushworth, who had been at great pains to learn all that the housekeeper could teach, and was now almost equally well qualified to shew the house. On the present occasion, she addressed herself chiefly to Miss Crawford and Fanny, but there was no comparison in the willingness of their attention, for Miss Crawford, who had seen scores of great houses, and cared for none of them, had only the appearance of civilly listening, while Fanny, to whom every thing was almost as interesting as it was new, attended with unaffected earnestness to all that Mrs. Rushworth could relate of the family in former times, its rise and grandeur, regal visits and loyal efforts, delighted to connect any thing with history already known, or warm her imagination with scenes of the past.</blockquote><br /><br />Fanny’s Cowper-quoting poetic nostalgia for ancient aristocratic domains may seem weird and fusty and reactionary, but it allows her imaginative access to that pageant “of the family in former times”—allows her a more textured experience of reality than is available to blasé Mary, for whom county houses are nothing more than the jaunty resorts of golden youth, evanescent as the entertainments and casual love-making they host. The contrast of Fanny and Mary in these chapters nicely demonstrates Pushkin’s observation that country distances, the solitude of estates, and the necessity of bookish diversion permit the provincial young lady to develop a pleasingly eccentric distinction of character, whereas <br /><br /><blockquote>in the capitol cities women receive a better education, perhaps, but the ways of society soon iron out their character and render their minds as indistinguishable as their hats.</blockquote><br /><br /><br /><br /><strong>Fanny Price</strong><br /><br /><br />Fanny is a stunning work of psychological portraiture. Austen knows and can show how an anxious ward acts and feels, in a convincing diversity of situation, down to the merest sigh and blushing demurral. Fanny isn’t a dazzling heroine—she has none of the “good humor of success”—but her peculiar blend of yielding meekness and steely conviction is so formidably imagined that she becomes, well, dazzling. The only touch of realism I missed, for a time, was her lack of a scapegoat. I had a hard time suppressing a knowing smirk when Austen first asked me to believe that the awful drama of displacement, the dread pecking order of distressed females should end at Fanny. I wanted one or two subtle, easy-to-miss scenes in which Fanny, in an extremity of anxiety, instinctively buffers her precarious status by kicking a little dirt on the dignity of some cringing chambermaid or under-butler. She wouldn’t even have to be mean about it, just helplessly, thoughtlessly seeking vent. <br /><br /><br />Three developments altered this line of thought. First, as Mary Crawford’s facile archness became more apparent, I realized I was as guilty as she in thinking that a cynical maxim—victims become victimizers—was sufficient to explain all being. I heard Jane from across the chasm of years, saying: <em>open your heart to the possibility of goodness, man!</em><br /><br /><br />Second, Fanny began to remind me of my girlfriend. Growing up she was a meek mouse amidst a volcanic father and three tyrannical thunder-lizard older sisters (weirdly Jurassic imagery, but I’ll go with it). She hid in her room hoping to be left alone. Like Fanny, her greatest fears were “of doing wrong and being looked at”—the phrase exactly describes her childhood, and at times her adulthood. She has Fanny’s mixture of over-scrupulous, accommodating tact and unexpected uncompromising obstinacy. And just as many readers say of Fanny, my girlfriend’s sisters complain that she’s self-righteous—which seems a weak charge, given that she knows all her sisters’ sources of pain and rage and could play on them if that sort of thing amused her. The realization of a flesh-and-blood Fanny analogue that doesn’t need to inflict revenge or displace grievance onto someone more vulnerable did much to suppress my initial, knowing suspicion of Fanny. <br /><br /><br />And third, I came to this passage:<br /><br /><blockquote>Fanny's disposition was such that she could never even think of her aunt Norris in the meagreness and cheerlessness of her own small house, without reproaching herself for some little want of attention to her when they had been last together...</blockquote><br /><br />Wow! So Fanny retains a reserve of compassion for her arch-tormentor, Mrs. Norris. That’s impressive. <em>They know not what they do</em>, for real. Like my girlfriend before her sisters, Fanny is more struck by the weakness that gives rise to bullying, than by the bullying itself. Fanny’s goodness is finally, completely plausible when we remember that she doesn’t see herself as a victim with a grievance to pass on, doesn’t see her tormentors as Goliaths deserving retaliation, direct or indirect. Mrs. Norris never looms too frightfully in Fanny’s imagination because as horrible as she may seek to be, to Fanny she is just a poor, lonely old lady, struggling to get by, and psychologically unequipped to cope civilly with hardship. Mrs. Norris’ bullying reveals a weakness, a wound to be tended, and a pitiable humanity.<br /><br /><br />Louis Auchincloss, who as a WWII naval officer regretted not bringing a set of Austen on his long voyages, said that a great theme of Austen’s work is “moral beauty” as it occurs in dissimilar heroines. Fanny’s “moral beauty,” by which phrase I take Auchincloss to mean her empathic respect of the reality and vulnerability of other people—how many times is someone humiliated, made abject, with Fanny, alone among the witnesses, fully registering the emotional shock?—occurs in the teeth of circumstances we expect to inhibit the expression of such a beauty. Fanny has every expected, every natural excuse to behave like her aunt Norris and harried mother, individuals whose attention and conversation—whose humanity, essentially—have been careworn down to monomaniacal browbeating of immediate inferiors. Instead Fanny makes her position near the bottom of the heap the ground of empathy, the ground of humane attention and do-unto-others. Trilling says we no longer trust the sickly-saintly quietist Christian heroine. But we should. She’s an undeniable instance of the moral life surviving social life.<br /><br /><br /></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="925820328358962598">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <a href="/user/show/79550-holly" class="left imgcol" title="Holly"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1178916176p2/79550.jpg" alt="Holly" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">

      <a href="/user/show/79550-holly" class="user" title="Holly">Holly</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/45032.Mansfield_Park" title="Paperback 9780141439808" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Austen fans, regency fans</span></div>

        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/79550-holly?shelf=classics" class="actionLinkLite">classics</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/79550-holly?shelf=female-writers" class="actionLinkLite">female-writers</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer5023565">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7683333365340799793">I'm really not surprised that not a lot of people like Fanny Price. She's timid, moralistic and extremely passive. But really, what were people expecting her to do, exactly? Tell her cousin she loves him? B-slap Miss Crawford? Fanny is low in society, brought up to be grateful to everyone, and has no independence (dowry, etc,.). A lot of women were like that in those days. Many shy people also have a higher regard for authority than others, because of authority's 'better' judgement, and that is</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText7683333365340799793">I'm really not surprised that not a lot of people like Fanny Price. She's timid, moralistic and extremely passive. But really, what were people expecting her to do, exactly? Tell her cousin she loves him? B-slap Miss Crawford? Fanny is low in society, brought up to be grateful to everyone, and has no independence (dowry, etc,.). A lot of women were like that in those days. Many shy people also have a higher regard for authority than others, because of authority's 'better' judgement, and that is why she's so unwilling to go against anyone's wishes. She is determined to please everyone, and therefore is unable to truly please herself. <br /><br />Fanny's story is one we all actually know - one of those situations in life where we have no power to do anything but watch. This is symbolised in the book when the other characters decide to start a play and Fanny refuses to take part; it is her status and her morals that prevent her from interferring in the plot, much as her being unable to act and unable to disobey her uncle stops her from joining in the play. <br /><br />She is moralistic - but she sticks to her morals, which is not something that can be called easy if it means having to disregard your own happiness sometimes. <br /><br />Personally, I think Fanny is one of Austen's most realistic heroines. Her flaws render her powerless. I did however resent the ending where Edmund suddenly realises he is in love with her and they get married. A bit too Harry Potter/Ginny Weasley for my liking (and I hated <em>that</em> relationship!). I would've preferred Fanny coming round to marrying Mr Crawford.</span>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_25086623">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/25086623" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/1213607-julie" class="left imgcol" title="Julie"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1445900882p2/1213607.jpg" alt="Julie" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/25086623?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Dec 04, 2015</a>

      <a href="/user/show/1213607-julie" class="user" title="Julie">Julie</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/11758562-mansfield-park" title="Hardcover 9780141197708" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/1213607-julie?shelf=classic" class="actionLinkLite">classic</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/1213607-julie?shelf=treasured-read-adult" class="actionLinkLite">treasured-read-adult</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/1213607-julie?shelf=british-isles-theme-setting" class="actionLinkLite">british-isles-theme-setting</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/1213607-julie?shelf=read-2015" class="actionLinkLite">read-2015</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer25086623">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10156175717138169287">The filling of the reading sandwich between my first time with <em>Mansfield Park</em> ten years ago and last week is Susan Cain's <em>Quiet: The Power of Introverts in a World That Can't Stop Talking</em>, which I happened upon two years ago. Cain's book was a revelation to me. At last, I finally understood my essence—after years of wondering what's wrong with me, why I crave so much time alone, why gatherings of people exhaust me, why, yes indeed, I steer my grocery cart abruptly away if I see someone I know in</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText10156175717138169287">The filling of the reading sandwich between my first time with <em>Mansfield Park</em> ten years ago and last week is Susan Cain's <em>Quiet: The Power of Introverts in a World That Can't Stop Talking</em>, which I happened upon two years ago. Cain's book was a revelation to me. At last, I finally understood my essence—after years of wondering what's wrong with me, why I crave so much time alone, why gatherings of people exhaust me, why, yes indeed, I steer my grocery cart abruptly away if I see someone I know in the aisle, simply to avoid the drudgery of small talk. Ain't <em>nothing</em> the matter with me. I'm an introvert. Friendly, not shy, at ease with public speaking, but classic introvert nonetheless. <br /><br />Fanny Price. I get you. While the rest of the world seems annoyed by your reticence, your reserved nature, your seemingly quiet acquiescence, I see a kindred spirit. You'd much rather be out on a solitary walk through the grounds of Mansfield Park, or talking to your horse, than sitting in the drawing room, suffering through endless games of whist and idle gossip. And when you love, you love with a singular focus. You don't need anyone else around; your heart can be filled with few souls-a beloved brother, a trusted friend. Too many chattering voices and you lose all focus . . . yet you're a dreamer. It's simply easiest to go along and pretend to give in to others' whims, for then you'll be left alone, able to return to your inner world. <br /><br />And yet, retreating does not mean you are ignorant of the greater events transpiring around you; the massive social changes ripping apart the seams of the rarefied fabric upholstering your adopted home. You question your bloviating uncle, Sir Thomas Bertram, about his affairs in Antigua, which is a euphemistic stand-in for the slave trade, (abolished in England in 1808, a few years before Austen wrote <em>Mansfield Park</em>, but slavery itself would not be outlawed until 1833, sixteen years after her death).<br /><br />You see right through Henry Crawford's cheap imitation of life, his pretty words that are no deeper than a coating of paint, yet your tender heart falls more readily for the promise of having just <em>
  <strong>one</strong>
</em> BFF when Mary Crawford showers you with attention. But, as is custom for introverts, nearly all people prove to be predictably disappointing in the end. It's better just to be alone. I know you have a horse, but really, a dog would suit you at least as well. A great companion for all those solitary walks in the woods outside Mansfield Park. <br /><br />This being Jane Austen, and Regency literature being what it is, there's got to be male yang to your female yin. And God love Edmund. A more perfect partner could not be found. He's quite the feminist, really; an introvert himself, a good egg, in training for the clergy. Steady, a bit dull, rather daft in matters of the heart, but eventually he gets there. You and he are the straight men in this social satire, reflecting the imperialistic privilege built on the backs of the underclass and the faraway slaves. You're both a bit moralistic and tedious, but at your cores, you are deeply conscious and conscientious. I think you will go on to do great things together. Social Justice Warriors of Regency England. I'm sure of it. And not a stage play in sight. <br /><br />So, I raise my previous 4-star rating up a notch, because I get you, Fanny. I forgive you, because I've finally forgiven myself. <br /><br />And Mrs. Norris? She's become my favorite Austen scoundrel. Best smackdown in all of Austen's oeuvre. <br /></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="10156175717138169287">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review nosyndicate" id="review_78493359">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/78493359" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/162759-margaret" class="left imgcol" title="Margaret"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1234732285p2/162759.jpg" alt="Margaret" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/78493359?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Jan 03, 2013</a>

      <a href="/user/show/162759-margaret" class="user" title="Margaret">Margaret</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/414986.Mansfield_Park" title="Hardcover 9780679412694" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/162759-margaret?shelf=british-literature" class="actionLinkLite">british-literature</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/162759-margaret?shelf=favorites" class="actionLinkLite">favorites</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/162759-margaret?shelf=austen" class="actionLinkLite">austen</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/162759-margaret?shelf=authors-ab" class="actionLinkLite">authors-ab</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer78493359">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1004713606178578710"><em>Mansfield Park</em> is perhaps not the one of Austen's novels which appeals the most to modern sensibilities; after all, reasonably faithful adaptations have been made recently of several of Austen's other novels, while <em>Mansfield Park</em> was changed into something Austen lovers barely recognized. Mansfield Park is the home of Fanny Price, the poor relation of Sir Thomas and Lady Bertram (Fanny's mother's sister), who took her to live with them from her impoverished Portsmouth home; Fanny is largely over</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText1004713606178578710"><em>Mansfield Park</em> is perhaps not the one of Austen's novels which appeals the most to modern sensibilities; after all, reasonably faithful adaptations have been made recently of several of Austen's other novels, while <em>Mansfield Park</em> was changed into something Austen lovers barely recognized. Mansfield Park is the home of Fanny Price, the poor relation of Sir Thomas and Lady Bertram (Fanny's mother's sister), who took her to live with them from her impoverished Portsmouth home; Fanny is largely overlooked and taken for granted by the Bertrams, her other aunt Mrs. Norris, and the Bertram children, but she finds solace in the friendship of her cousin Edmund Bertram. When the Crawford siblings, Henry and Mary, come to Mansfield parsonage to stay with their sister, the wife of the clergyman Dr. Grant, they unsettle Mansfield society with gay doings and flirtations which lead to more serious events.<br /><br />Fanny is self-effacing to the point of passivity, in marked contrast to Austen's more lively heroines, like Elizabeth Bennet of <em>Pride and Prejudice</em> or Emma Woodhouse of <em>Emma</em>, which I think is one reason <em>Mansfield Park</em> is somewhat difficult to like on first reading (and why it was changed so drastically for the film version); yet her moral sense and voice pervade <em>Mansfield Park</em>, and gradually, one grows to realize that she is a woman of deep convictions. When the others decide to put on a play of dubious moral quality and even Edmund joins in, Fanny resists everyone's blandishments to persuade her to take part; when Sir Thomas tries to convince her to marry a man she doesn't love, she resists that as well. She's no Lizzy, but she holds fast to her beliefs more than anyone else in this novel and emerges as a truly worthy heroine.<br /><br />I wish that Austen had seen fit to match Fanny with a more interesting hero, but I guess you can't have everything. <em>Mansfield Park</em> does have much else to savor: the brilliant episode of the play-acting and the scenes at Portsmouth, unlike anything else Austen depicted in their portrait of family life among the not-so-well-off, are particularly masterly. It may be slower than some of the other novels, but <em>Mansfield Park</em> is one of the deepest and most rewarding of Austen's books.<br /></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="1004713606178578710">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_92733718">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/92733718" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/954028-deborah-markus" class="left imgcol" title="Deborah Markus"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1442990055p2/954028.jpg" alt="Deborah Markus" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/92733718?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Dec 09, 2014</a>

      <a href="/user/show/954028-deborah-markus" class="user" title="Deborah Markus">Deborah Markus</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/427640.Mansfield_Park" title="Paperback 9780393967913" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer92733718">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3190092716593222080"><em>Mansfield Park</em> is probably Austen's least liked novel. <em>Northanger Abbey</em> may be flawed, but it's a romp and a quick read; <em>Persuasion</em> may be dark, but it's tender and passionate, and contains quite possibly Austen's greatest proposal ever. <br /><br />But what does <em>Mansfield Park</em> have to offer? A heroine who possesses every 18th-century feminine virtue? Hardly a recommendation to a 21st-century reader. A main character so physically delicate one can hardly imagine her surviving her wedding night, let alone ch</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText3190092716593222080"><em>Mansfield Park</em> is probably Austen's least liked novel. <em>Northanger Abbey</em> may be flawed, but it's a romp and a quick read; <em>Persuasion</em> may be dark, but it's tender and passionate, and contains quite possibly Austen's greatest proposal ever. <br /><br />But what does <em>Mansfield Park</em> have to offer? A heroine who possesses every 18th-century feminine virtue? Hardly a recommendation to a 21st-century reader. A main character so physically delicate one can hardly imagine her surviving her wedding night, let alone childbirth? Again -- no thanks. A girl who spends the novel being miserable when those around her are happy and vice versa? Problematic at best.<br /><br />None of which explains why I've read this book so often, and why I prefer it to <em>Persuasion.</em> <br /><br />This edition is superb. The accompanying essays are well chosen and illuminating. I found Joseph Lew's <em>"Mansfield Park</em> And The Dynamics of Slavery" especially valuable.<br /><br /><strong>UPDATE: Why the heck does Deborah like this unlikable book so much?</strong><br /><br />Fanny Price is in many ways a difficult heroine for most of us to love or relate to. She is ridiculously timid and fragile. She meekly accepts whatever's handed to her, and then apologizes for having taken too much. She's sometimes preachy. And, worst of all, she reads a lot, but <em>never novels.</em> (shudder)<br /><br />The guy she falls for is equally unattractive. I'm willing to bet actual physical money that no Austen fan has <em>ever</em> had a crush on Edmund Bertram. He's often pompous, occasionally hypocritical, and breathtakingly oblivious of the GINORMOUS crush Fanny's had on him since she was transplanted from her parents' home to his at an early age. Also, he's her first cousin, and while it was perfectly acceptable for such relations to marry at the time, it's a little skeevy for many modern readers.<br /><br />Other Austen heroines have some adventures, or at least some fun: they travel, have picnics, go dancing, ride with cute guys in carriages, and attend parties. Fanny Price rarely does anything at all, and when she does it's generally a disaster. Her happiest days are the ones where she's left entirely alone and unharassed to read or sew in the cold, isolated room she's allowed to call her own (because it's too uncomfortable for anyone else to want).<br /><br />And then, to top it all off, when a rich amoral rake gets a crush on her, everyone else approves of his relentless pursuit of Fanny and she's condemned for protesting that she simply can't give herself to such a man.<br /><br />So why the heck do I reread this book so often?<br /><br />I think because in many ways, this is a very <em>rich</em> novel -- the closest Austen ever comes to an epic family saga. It begins before Fanny is even born, and so we're given the only glimpse ever seen of an Austen heroine as a child.<br /><br />There's also a great deal of very convincing conversation, a lot of it about privilege and power. I enjoy that kind of thing. <br /><br />And Mary Crawford, a sort of anti-heroine, is a terrifyingly attractive character. How on earth can Fanny Price stand a chance against her?<br /><br />Fanny Price is physically weak, but she's the only character who has a functioning moral compass, and is ultimately incorruptible. She is genuinely virtuous. But she's also human. She struggles. She isn't effortlessly perfect. She is sometimes angry with those around her for not seeing the harm they're doing.<br /><br />She suffers more than any Austen heroine, and at one point is in real danger. But her sorrows and struggles are convincing, rather than 18th-century melodrama. Some of them are all too easy to relate to.<br /><br />Also, the characterization is fantastic. Fanny's father is brilliantly unlike any other Austen character. Mary Crawford is chillingly like Austen's <em>Lady Susan.</em> And her brother -- well, his pursuit of Fanny Price is, I must admit, kind of creepy and sexy at the same time. The intensity of his desire not just to marry but to <em>conquer</em> her is darkly sexual. I'm sure there are about 7 million naughty fan-fiction stories out there based on the premise of his winning this erotic struggle.<br /><br />So, yes, this book is hard to love. But I think it's worth the work. I adored Fanny Price the first time I read it; despised her the second time through; and now have a reasonable affection for her -- and an unreasonable admiration for this novel.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="3190092716593222080">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer13771083443075035357">Argh! I am very surprised that I was able to finish this book. I found it to be completely tedious. If this had been the first book I had read by Jane Austen, it would have been enough to turn me off from the rest of her works.<br /><br />Fanny was too virtuous a character to be likable. She was dull as powder and entirely too submissive. I would have much more enjoyed reading about the spunky Miss Mary Crawford.   <span style="display:none" id="freeText13771083443075035357">Argh! I am very surprised that I was able to finish this book. I found it to be completely tedious. If this had been the first book I had read by Jane Austen, it would have been enough to turn me off from the rest of her works.<br /><br />Fanny was too virtuous a character to be likable. She was dull as powder and entirely too submissive. I would have much more enjoyed reading about the spunky Miss Mary Crawford. She would have proved to be a more approachable heroine and her high jinx could never be boring.<br /><br />I have seen several reviews referring to a character named Edward. Huh? Wasn't his name Edmund?? Was I reading the same book? Talk about your forgettable characters. <br /><br />The shining star in this book was Mrs. Norris...probably the LEAST likeable character. She is the epitome of 'characters you love to hate'. Reading about her antics was the saving grace in this otherwise vanilla novel...much like reading the parts about Satan in <em>Paradise Lost</em>. Villains add depth.<br /><br />However, Mrs. Norris couldn't entirely save this one for me. 2 stars...at best.</span>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review nosyndicate" id="review_168109889">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/168109889" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/4542979-kim" class="left imgcol" title="Kim"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1451622200p2/4542979.jpg" alt="Kim" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/168109889?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Nov 25, 2011</a>

      <a href="/user/show/4542979-kim" class="user" title="Kim">Kim</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/915916.Mansfield_Park" title="Audio CD 9781572708600" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/4542979-kim?shelf=audiobook" class="actionLinkLite">audiobook</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/4542979-kim?shelf=re-read" class="actionLinkLite">re-read</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer168109889">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17824392437099795647"><br /><strong>This review contains some spoilers.</strong><br /><br />About a year ago I started a Jane Austen project, which has involved listening to the six major novels on audiobook, most of them narrated by Juliet Stevenson, who is simply wonderful at bringing Austen’s characters to life. It says something about me that in this period I have listened to <a title="Persuasion by Jane Austen" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/2156.Persuasion">Persuasion</a> twice. It says something else about me that I left this book until last. That I did so won't come as a big surprise to admirers of Jane Austen's novels. This is th</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText17824392437099795647"><br /><strong>This review contains some spoilers.</strong><br /><br />About a year ago I started a Jane Austen project, which has involved listening to the six major novels on audiobook, most of them narrated by Juliet Stevenson, who is simply wonderful at bringing Austen’s characters to life. It says something about me that in this period I have listened to <a title="Persuasion by Jane Austen" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/2156.Persuasion">Persuasion</a> twice. It says something else about me that I left this book until last. That I did so won't come as a big surprise to admirers of Jane Austen's novels. This is the novel which is most likely to be disliked, even by readers who would otherwise call themselves fans of the author. That includes me. I have read Jane Austen’s other novels innumerable times, but this one only twice before: once at school and once at university. Until this week it was not a novel that I would have described in positive terms.<br /><br />One of the main reasons for the unpopularity of <em>Mansfield Park</em> is the heroine, Fanny Price. A lot of what is perceived to be wrong with Fanny comes from the fact she is so unlike other Austen heroines. She lacks the wit and liveliness of Elizabeth Bennett, the warmth of Anne Elliot, the energy of Elinor Dashwood, the passion of Marianne Dashwood, the self-confidence of Emma Woodhouse, the youthful high spirits of Catherine Morland. Fanny is shy, quiet, nervous, physically weak and very serious. She has a strong sense of right and wrong. She lacks a sense of humour and many would describe her as unbearably self-righteous and a prig.<br /><br />Fanny’s character has always divided opinion. According to Austen’s biographer <a title="Claire Tomalin" href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/18514.Claire_Tomalin">Claire Tomalin</a>, Austen’s mother thought that Fanny was insipid. Austen’s niece and one of her nephews couldn’t bear her. Her sister Cassandra was fond of Fanny but wanted her to marry Henry Crawford. So, the problems with Fanny as a character are not merely a result of her moral principles being at odds with a modern sensibility. Fanny is just not a good fit with the Austen heroines readers know and love.<br /><br />However, even though Fanny has some unattractive personality traits, she is still a very interesting character. It’s not hard to see the scared, lonely and frightened little girl in the reserved young woman she becomes. In addition, it’s clear that Fanny’s dislike of Henry and Mary Crawford is not just because of her good principles. It’s also because she is in love with Edmund (and therefore can’t love Henry) and Edmund loves Mary (so Fanny is jealous of her). So while Fanny proves to be a good judge of character, she also has her very human flaws.<br /><br />My own response to Fanny shifted back and forward during the novel. I felt for the unloved child, but I wanted to shake the passive young woman. I understood her love for Edmund and I wished she liked Henry. She would annoy me and then say something profound. My reaction to the other characters was also complex. I loved Edmund’s care and compassion for the young Fanny, but found his self-righteousness annoying. I was charmed by the Crawfords, but deeply aware of their very considerable flaws. There was nothing complex about my response to Mrs Norris and Lady Bertram, though. Mrs Norris’ malevolence and Lady Bertram’s stupidity are worth the purchase price of the book. <br /><br />There’s so much more to say about <em>Mansfield Park</em> than I have written in this review. Maybe the best thing I can say is this: it is a novel which has made me think a lot, both as I was listening to it and since. That’s not my usual reaction to Austen. Normally, I read Austen and simply enjoy the writing, the characters and the wit. This time was different. I found <em>Mansfield Park</em> interesting, engaging, frustrating and puzzling. It’s gone up to a four star read and I won’t be waiting another thirty years before I read it again. <br /></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="17824392437099795647">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span style="display:none" id="freeText9805175499339755888">It seems that we came to similar conclusions but your review has a great deal more depth than mine. I'm glad that some of us are standing up for Fanny. If nothing else, she represents a powerful example of the power of the environment to shape a person's character. I particularly liked the contrast between Lady Bertram and Mrs Price. This is a cautionary tale for those who believe that true love trumps more practical considerations. After having read Thomas Hardy over the summer, it is interesting to read Jane Austen again. She had such an influence on his development. Maybe it's time for me to revisit Persuasion.</span>

  <span style="display:none" id="freeText12885491891160505761"><em>Gary wrote: "This is a cautionary tale for those who believe that true love trumps more practical considerations. They see Austen as someone who wrote romances and can't come to grips with a novel in which the most stereotypically romantic characters - the Crawfords - come to no good. I hadn't thought about Austen being an influence on Hardy, but I can see that it must be so, although he wrote about a different social class and the natural world plays much more of a part in his work. <br /><br />As for Persuasion, I don't think it can be revisited too often!</span>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/15538444?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Feb 22, 2008</a>

      <a href="/user/show/592708-oceana9" class="user" title="Oceana9">Oceana9</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>

          <a href="/book/show/45032.Mansfield_Park" title="Paperback 9780141439808" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Anyone who can stand nineteenth-century British novelists. </span></div>

        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer15538444">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10795745249813259156">Fanny Price. Yes, the protagonist's name is really Fanny Price. This book is uber-Austen, so expect all the Austenish things: a saintly heroine, clever talkin', love triangles and love squares. Much walking amongst the shrubbery. Letter-writin'. Good brother vs. bad brother. (Both are hot.) Evil rich sisters (both are hot.) Poor, destitute cousin taken in by "charitable" impulses (Fanny is not hot, but then, of course, gets hotter and hotter as people begin to notice her. She is hottest when blu</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText10795745249813259156">Fanny Price. Yes, the protagonist's name is really Fanny Price. This book is uber-Austen, so expect all the Austenish things: a saintly heroine, clever talkin', love triangles and love squares. Much walking amongst the shrubbery. Letter-writin'. Good brother vs. bad brother. (Both are hot.) Evil rich sisters (both are hot.) Poor, destitute cousin taken in by "charitable" impulses (Fanny is not hot, but then, of course, gets hotter and hotter as people begin to notice her. She is hottest when blushing, hanging out with her beloved sailor brother [sooooo hot], and showing her unbending virtue, set off against the fallen crowd of richies.)<br /><br />As much as it annoyed me in all its predictable ravelings and unravelings, I fell in love with Fanny Price--I think I might make a T-shirt--and I think some of these savvy, hip, cynical modern novelists (OK, now I sound like I'm 75) could stand a a dose of good old Austenish critiquing. Materialism, snobbishness, cattiness, elitism, selfishness, cruelty: she takes 'em all on. But for the fake boobs, Mansfield Park might as well be Newport Beach.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="10795745249813259156">...more</a>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review nosyndicate" id="review_11983880">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/11983880" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/285565-rebecca" class="left imgcol" title="Rebecca"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1315260462p2/285565.jpg" alt="Rebecca" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/11983880?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Jan 17, 2008</a>

      <a href="/user/show/285565-rebecca" class="user" title="Rebecca">Rebecca</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was ok">it was ok</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/45032.Mansfield_Park" title="Paperback 9780141439808" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Brit lit lovers</span></div>

        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer11983880">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1709661009904673497">I don't think this book would have been so disappointing if I hadn't just seen the movie adaptation of it (specifically, the 1999 version). I saw the movie first, and liked the plot so much that I started the book. I enjoyed reading <em>P &amp; P</em> and <em>S &amp; S</em>, so I assumed I would enjoy <em>Mansfield Park</em> also. I quickly found out that the movie was much more entertaining -- but more importantly than that, its social/political message was more palatable to me than the book's.<br /><br />In the movie, the protagoni</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText1709661009904673497">I don't think this book would have been so disappointing if I hadn't just seen the movie adaptation of it (specifically, the 1999 version). I saw the movie first, and liked the plot so much that I started the book. I enjoyed reading <em>P &amp; P</em> and <em>S &amp; S</em>, so I assumed I would enjoy <em>Mansfield Park</em> also. I quickly found out that the movie was much more entertaining -- but more importantly than that, its social/political message was more palatable to me than the book's.<br /><br />In the movie, the protagonist, Fanny Price, is a spirited, independent-minded, witty, and fiercely smart girl of 18 or so who loves reading and has a secret crush on her cousin Edmund. Everyone around her is vain, selfish, and superficial, but she remains a voice of reason and kindness throughout. In the end, of course, she gets Edmund and lives happily ever after.<br /><br />In the book, Fanny does still get the man and sees her foolish relatives get their just desserts, but her personality is so different from the Fanny of the movie that I felt a bit cheated and deprived. It quickly became apparent that the movie had taken radical liberties with Fanny's character -- but they were liberties that I felt improved the story and maybe they were necessary for a '90s audience. At least, they felt necessary to me.<br /><br />The Fanny of the book is subservient, repressed, and suffers from "nerves," to the point that I could not respect or like her. Her "virtue" (so Austen calls it) is in putting everyone's needs and desires (as stupid as they may be) ahead of her own welfare and happiness. Austen obviously came from a time when women were idealized as being domestic angels that were meant to serve and be silent, but I had had higher hopes for Austen's subversive potential. Not only was Fanny unrealistically pure and virtuous, she let others walk all over her, even when speaking her mind would have been justified. The narrator also repeatedly described Fanny's frailty, mentioning several times that strolling leisurely for more than an hour was enough to make Fanny exhausted and completely dependent on the nearest male, lest she collapse in the garden. In addition, Fanny's intellect and interest in reading is downplayed, and her time appears to be spent sewing, talking, and taking exhausting strolls in gardens. In other words, she has a completely bland and prudish personality that only distinguishes itself in contrast to her reprehensible relatives.<br /><br />Overall, Austen's message seems to be: "If you neglect your own health and happiness for long enough, in the name of serving and submitting to those who consider themselves your superiors, you will eventually be rewarded by a decent income and a handsome husband."<br /><br />One other change made in the movie, in addition to Fanny's character, is the nature of slavery. In the book, very slight mention is made of the fact that Fanny's uncle and guardian owns slaves in Antigua (in fact, Austen never uses the word "slave," you just have to infer). No mention beyond this is made of the issue. In the movie, however, Fanny comes across some of her uncle's notes about the plantation and is morally outraged, and in the end, her uncle (partially because of her influence), decides to give up his plantation. This was totally absent from the book, and is yet another example of Austen's claustrophobic and circumscribed focus on domestic and trivial issues to the exclusion of significant historical factors that should influence her character's lives, but don't. <br /><br />I must admit that this book has lowered my regard for Jane Austen, but that aside, it has several funny and touching moments, and has some of the best insights into human stupidity that I've ever read, making it well worth a read by anyone. </span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="1709661009904673497">...more</a>

          </span>

<span id="freeTextContainer2623565355735248901">I can not agree with you about losing respect for Jane Austen because of Fanny. If anything, Fanny Price's characterization proves JA's deep understan</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText2623565355735248901">I can not agree with you about losing respect for Jane Austen because of Fanny. If anything, Fanny Price's characterization proves JA's deep understanding of Human Nature. Fanny Price is one the the most realistic abuse survivors in Literature. Given away as a child (to her young mind), then either neglected or verbally abused by people she is TAUGHT EARLY she needs to please....with no chance or returning home...no profession or idea of working..Such A situation does not produce an Elizabeth Bennet or Emma Woodhouse. Both had at least one loving parent. Edmund, only home for school holidays, can't work miracles. With the severe abuse Fanny receives from Aunt Norris (Last and Lowest) and everything in her life affirming that its no wonder she's passive with a rich inner life. Today she would be called the Invisible Child. I personally really like her. She and Celie from The Color Purple are amazingly accurate portraits of abuse.</span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_1254315818">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1254315818" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/2457095-apatt" class="left imgcol" title="Apatt"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1349239948p2/2457095.jpg" alt="Apatt" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/1254315818?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">May 02, 2015</a>

      <a href="/user/show/2457095-apatt" class="user" title="Apatt">Apatt</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/45032.Mansfield_Park" title="Paperback 9780141439808" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/2457095-apatt?shelf=classics" class="actionLinkLite">classics</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/2457095-apatt?shelf=fave-classics" class="actionLinkLite">fave-classics</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer1254315818">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14423452006786047891">For some reason I keep coming back to read Jane Austen in spite of liking only two of the five* that I have read previous to <em>Mansfield Park</em>. I love her prose and dialogues but her tales of “conjugal felicity” are usually less than riveting for me. Still, I keep coming back for more of her romantic shenanigans so I guess – for me – her prose is more important than her plot at least where Austen is concerned.<br /><br />So I started <em>Mansfield Park</em> with some trepidation, once again wondering why I bother. The</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText14423452006786047891">For some reason I keep coming back to read Jane Austen in spite of liking only two of the five* that I have read previous to <em>Mansfield Park</em>. I love her prose and dialogues but her tales of “conjugal felicity” are usually less than riveting for me. Still, I keep coming back for more of her romantic shenanigans so I guess – for me – her prose is more important than her plot at least where Austen is concerned.<br /><br />So I started <em>Mansfield Park</em> with some trepidation, once again wondering why I bother. The Telegraph’s declaration that this is <a rel="nofollow" href="http://www.telegraph.co.uk/culture/books/10987048/Mansfield-Park-shows-the-dark-side-of-Jane-Austen.html"> “Jane Austen’s least-loved novel”</a> did not bode well for me, but lo and behold!, as early as Chapter One I found myself digging the book, in other words I was happy to give it my complete approbation. “Least-loved novel”? Tcha! Upon my word! etc. I think with this book Austen was firing from all cylinders, telling an interesting and psychologically complex yarn through her reliably gorgeous prose.<br /><br />Like all Jane Austen novels <em>Mansfield Park</em> focuses on a single female protagonist, Miss Fanny Price on this occasion. What confounded my expectation immediately is that her childhood in the first chapter is one of privation. When she moves from her crappy family home to live with her rich aunt she is a fish out of water and regarded by the residents of her aunt’s house (the eponymous Mansfield Park) as something of a plebeian; what with her inability to play any musical instrument and ignorance of geography etc. The only kindness she receives is from super nice boy Edmund Bertram who can immediately be identified as the book’s romantic interest. Still the circuitous route to the eventual romantic relationship is even more circuitous than usual. Fanny Price has a much harder time of it than other Austen heroines, though <a title="Thomas Hardy" href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/15905.Thomas_Hardy"> Thomas Hardy</a>’s heroines would have scoffed at her difficulties.<br /><br /><em>Mansfield Park</em> is populated with quite a few colorful characters, there is the womanizing cad Henry Crawford who is a stock Austen antagoist, his sister Mary who is an evil sultry seductress, a nasty <s>b</s>witchy aunt**, and Sir Thomas Bertram, a stick in the mud rich uncle. Interestingly the adjective “colorful” cannot be applied to the two main characters Fanny and Edmund. In fact this book’s unpopularity among die hard Janeites mostly stems from Fanny’s doormat-ish character, always shy and retiring, humble and repressed, the polar opposite of <a title="Pride and Prejudice by Jane Austen" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/1885.Pride_and_Prejudice"> Pride and Prejudice</a>’s Elizabeth Bennet. Yet, in her defence Fanny is sharp as a pin, she is more akin to <a title="Agatha Christie" href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/123715.Agatha_Christie"> Agatha Christies</a>’s Miss Marple than Liz Bennet. She can quickly and accurately sum up people’s true characters regardless of their surface attraction or amiability. This acute power of observation serves her well when practically all the characters in the book gang up on her to persuade or pressurize her into marrying the wealthy and slick Henry Crawford. Doormat or no she stands her ground and is completely vindicated by the end of the book.<br /><br /><em>“I think it ought not to be set down as certain that a man must be acceptable to every woman he may happen to like himself.”</em><br /><br />I think this is a key passage that nicely encapsulates her attitude.<br /><br />Much as I like the book I do find the ending quite predictable, Austen seems to always resort to an “elopement crisis” to create a climactic drama, and while there are some signs that Henry may reform through the love of a good woman he reverts back to his traditional Austen villain role. I would really like to see an unconventional ending to what is already an unconventional Austen adventure.<br /><br />Still I thoroughly enjoyed this book and can – for once – recommend it unconditionally.<br />___________________________<br /><br />* <a title="Pride and Prejudice by Jane Austen" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/1885.Pride_and_Prejudice"> Pride and Prejudice</a> (liked), <a title="Sense and Sensibility by Jane Austen" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/14935.Sense_and_Sensibility"> Sense and Sensibility</a> (liked), <a title="Emma by Jane Austen" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/6969.Emma"> Emma</a> ("as if!"), <a title="Persuasion by Jane Austen" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/2156.Persuasion"> Persuasion</a> (boring), <a title="Northanger Abbey by Jane Austen" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/50398.Northanger_Abbey"> Northanger Abbey</a> (boring).<br /><br />** Mrs. Norris is a nasty piece of work, but within Jane Austen's limited scope of nastiness. The worst thing I can imagine doing to an Austen villain is to waggle my finger at them with extreme prejudice.<br /><br /><u>Notes</u><br />I read/listened to the <a rel="nofollow" href="https://librivox.org/mansfield-park-by-jane-austen-version-2/">free audiobook version</a> beautifully read by Karen Savage, in her soothing and pretty voice. Thank you Ms. Savage.<br /><br /><u>Whovian corner</u> (dedicated to my dear friend Cecily)<br />Billie Piper as Fanny Price:<br /><img class="escapedImg" src="http://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1430293486i/14701613.jpg" /><br />‘Nuff said!</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="14423452006786047891">...more</a>

          </span>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_1263518508">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1263518508" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/5701086-branwen-sedai-of-the-white-ajah" class="left imgcol" title="Branwen Sedai *of the White Ajah*"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1450543698p2/5701086.jpg" alt="Branwen Sedai *of the White Ajah*" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/1263518508?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Jun 28, 2015</a>

      <a href="/user/show/5701086-branwen-sedai-of-the-white-ajah" class="user" title="Branwen Sedai *of the White Ajah*">Branwen Sedai *of the White Ajah*</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/11758562-mansfield-park" title="Hardcover 9780141197708" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/5701086-branwen-sedai-of-the-white-ajah?shelf=favorites" class="actionLinkLite">favorites</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer1263518508">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14457771249004467267"><em>"But then I am unlike other people, I dare say...."</em><br /><br /><a rel="nofollow" target="_blank" href="http://s24.photobucket.com/user/renamon_slave/media/fannyprice_zpsdtf4ycsd.jpg.html"> <img class="escapedImg" alt=" photo fannyprice_zpsdtf4ycsd.jpg" src="http://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1435542004i/15357929._SY540_.jpg" /></a><br /><br />Mansfield Park is my absolute <strong>favorite</strong> novel by Jane Austen. This may come as a bit of a surprise because generally it is considered her least popular work. And I can understand how many people could not like it, especially when compared with the heroines from Pride and Prejudice and Sense and Sensibility. Fanny Price is neither headstrong or sassy. She doesn't assert her will over others, possess a cool logical mind, or sass others. Many criti</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText14457771249004467267"><em>"But then I am unlike other people, I dare say...."</em><br /><br /><a rel="nofollow" target="_blank" href="http://s24.photobucket.com/user/renamon_slave/media/fannyprice_zpsdtf4ycsd.jpg.html">
  <img class="escapedImg" alt=" photo fannyprice_zpsdtf4ycsd.jpg" src="http://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1435542004i/15357929._SY540_.jpg" /></a><br /><br />Mansfield Park is my absolute <strong>favorite</strong> novel by Jane Austen. This may come as a bit of a surprise because generally it is considered her least popular work. And I can understand how many people could not like it, especially when compared with the heroines from Pride and Prejudice and Sense and Sensibility. Fanny Price is neither headstrong or sassy. She doesn't assert her will over others, possess a cool logical mind, or sass others. Many critics have labeled her a 'prig' because of her quiet and timid manner. But I felt something completely different when reading this book. I felt strength from Fanny Price. Yes, she is quiet and yes she is timid. But she is also; kind, compassionate, and steadfast. She is never cruel or malicious, even when members of her own family are treating her poorly. But her steadfastness is what really made me admire her profoundly. When pursued by eligible bachelor Henry Crawford, she sticks to her own will and refuses him, on numerous occasions. Despite his handsomeness and wealth, and despite the pressure on her from her family to marry him, she remains true to her heart and her will. And in the end, it completely pays off for her. So even though on the surface she may not seem as amazing as Lizzy or Elinor or Emma, or any of Austen's other heroines, Fanny remains number one in my book! :)</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="14457771249004467267">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

      <a href="/user/show/2569035-mark" class="user" title="Mark">Mark</a>

        rated it

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/2569035-mark?shelf=heroes-who-are-up-their-own-bums" class="actionLinkLite">heroes-who-are-up-their-own-bums</a>
          </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer75483333">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17252569615850993327">My wonderful English teacher at school, Mr Flint, encouraged us to always read around the writer. So at A'level, studying 'Emma' he urged us to read the other five Austens. <br /><br />Thus 'Mansfield Park' first read at 17. Fanny = Put upon heroine of epic proportions, noble, insightful, courageous and brave. Re-read at 32. Fanny = self righteous prig, judgemental little minx, singularly unattractive whinger. Another 15 years on and I opted for 'Murder at Mansfield Park' instead. <br /><br />(This results in something</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText17252569615850993327">My wonderful English teacher at school, Mr Flint, encouraged us to always read around the writer. So at A'level, studying 'Emma' he urged us to read the other five Austens. <br /><br />Thus 'Mansfield Park' first read at 17. Fanny = Put upon heroine of epic proportions, noble, insightful, courageous and brave. Re-read at 32. Fanny = self righteous prig, judgemental little minx, singularly unattractive whinger. Another 15 years on and I opted for 'Murder at Mansfield Park' instead. <br /><br />(This results in something smacking of 'Goldlilocks and the three bears' really, as in, <strong>"this Fanny was too good, this Fanny was too yeuch..... this Fanny was just right"</strong>)<br /><br />Going back to my first encounter, as Fanny was admired and feted in my 17 year old manifestation, this either speaks volumes for my insightful adolescent self or it amazes me that i was not beaten to death by those who had to share the planet with me at that time. <br /><br />As in the appalling 'Pearl Harbour' where Kate Beckinsale ends up with the wrong mate surviving to be her husband so I think it undoubtedly the case that Edmund ends up with the wrong girl. Mary Crawford forever I say though thinking about it, Edmund and Fanny ............perfectly suited in every lifeless, blood draining, mind-numbingly self-obsessed way..................I was not keen......Mary perhaps got off at just the right time.</span>

  <span style="display:none" id="freeText6450272238922725075">Henry, try 'Emma'. She is the character of whom Austen herself wrote 'I am going to create a heroine whom no-one but myself will very much like'. However, you will probably warm to her during the course of the book though she needs a good shaking most of the time.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="6450272238922725075">...more</a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/1062934887?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Sep 29, 2014</a>

      <a href="/user/show/4596358-ferdy" class="user" title="Ferdy">Ferdy</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was ok">it was ok</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/14391212-mansfield-park" title="Hardcover 9781444732566" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/4596358-ferdy?shelf=boring-crap-hero" class="actionLinkLite">boring-crap-hero</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/4596358-ferdy?shelf=doormat-heroine" class="actionLinkLite">doormat-heroine</a>,
