              <a href="/review/list/4596358-ferdy?shelf=blah-romance" class="actionLinkLite">blah-romance</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/4596358-ferdy?shelf=fucked-up-romance" class="actionLinkLite">fucked-up-romance</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/4596358-ferdy?shelf=incest" class="actionLinkLite">incest</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/4596358-ferdy?shelf=boring-main-characters" class="actionLinkLite">boring-main-characters</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/4596358-ferdy?shelf=unlikeable-characters" class="actionLinkLite">unlikeable-characters</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/4596358-ferdy?shelf=die-heroine-die" class="actionLinkLite">die-heroine-die</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/4596358-ferdy?shelf=classic" class="actionLinkLite">classic</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer1062934887">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2890167440696437545">My least favourite Austen novel. When I first read <em>Mansfield Park</em> years ago I thoroughly enjoyed it but on re-reading, I no longer felt the same. <br /><br />The main characters (Fanny/Edmund) were the worst, they were bloody awful, I can't believe I ever used to like them. I was more entertained by the secondary characters even though all of them were one dimensional and horrible, despite them being flat and unlikeable they still managed to be more endearing and entertaining than Fanny and Edmund. <br /><br />Fanny ha</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText2890167440696437545">My least favourite Austen novel. When I first read <em>Mansfield Park</em> years ago I thoroughly enjoyed it but on re-reading, I no longer felt the same. <br /><br />The main characters (Fanny/Edmund) were the worst, they were bloody awful, I can't believe I ever used to like them. I was more entertained by the secondary characters even though all of them were one dimensional and horrible, despite them being flat and unlikeable they still managed to be more endearing and entertaining than Fanny and Edmund. <br /><br />Fanny had a rubbish personality, she was judgemental, self-righteous, insipid, obedient, and always bloody tired. The girl couldn't walk or talk for five seconds without wanting to collapse, she was such a lazy cow. <br />I hated how everything in Fanny's life revolved around William and Edmund, she didn't care about anyone else, it was only her speshul men that mattered to her. There wasn't one female character Fanny genuinely admired, all of them were lacking in some way to her. She really was a stuck up bint.<br /><br />Edmund wasn't much better as a character, he was utterly unlikeable. I was really put off by his over-the-top strictness, prudishness and holier than thou attitude, not to mention his hypocrisy when it came to Mary or whatever her name was. His shock at Mary for supporting her cheating brother was ridiculous, she was hardly going to condemn and cut off the last family she had left. There was nothing wrong with her loyalty to her brother. She had a lucky escape when Edmund walked away from her. <br /><br />I'm usually pissed off at romances where the heroine is clearly the second choice, but I didn't care that Fanny ended up with Edmund even though it was obvious she was second best, and was only 'loved' and 'chosen' by him because she was obedient and already familiar to him and he couldn't be bothered finding real love again. The two of them ending up together wasn't romantic whatsoever but it was realistic since they were both boring and stuck up, they really did deserve each other.<br /><br />I can't believe I ever liked this, the story was dull and the main characters weren't engaging in any way. I'll only ever re-read it again if I find I'm having trouble sleeping.<br /></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="2890167440696437545">...more</a>

    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/167012018?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Mar 14, 2013</a>

      <a href="/user/show/3335664-david" class="user" title="David">David</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it">liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/2697019-mansfield-park" title="Audio CD 9780786162642" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Austen fans</span></div>

        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/3335664-david?shelf=classic" class="actionLinkLite">classic</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/3335664-david?shelf=literary" class="actionLinkLite">literary</a>,

              <a href="/review/list/3335664-david?shelf=british-literature" class="actionLinkLite">british-literature</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/3335664-david?shelf=female-author" class="actionLinkLite">female-author</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/3335664-david?shelf=regency" class="actionLinkLite">regency</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/3335664-david?shelf=19th-century" class="actionLinkLite">19th-century</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/3335664-david?shelf=england" class="actionLinkLite">england</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer167012018">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2402798688973928675">I have generally enjoyed my excursions into Austen-land, but if Austen were a modern writer, I'd call this book a sophomore slump. It was her third, and much of the plot and character interactions were very derivative of <a href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/133027802">Pride and Prejudice</a> but without that book's humor or sting.<br /><br />Fanny Price is one of a brood of children, and her mother sends her to live with wealthy relatives to relieve her own family of some of the burden. Fanny thus grows up as the "poor cousin" in a wealthy house, generally n</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText2402798688973928675">I have generally enjoyed my excursions into Austen-land, but if Austen were a modern writer, I'd call this book a sophomore slump. It was her third, and much of the plot and character interactions were very derivative of <a href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/133027802">Pride and Prejudice</a> but without that book's humor or sting.<br /><br />Fanny Price is one of a brood of children, and her mother sends her to live with wealthy relatives to relieve her own family of some of the burden. Fanny thus grows up as the "poor cousin" in a wealthy house, generally not ill-treated but she is constantly condescended to and slighted. The result is that Fanny is a shy, blushing, ridiculously self-depreciating creature whom I found hard to like, though we're obviously meant to. I should say, as a person I would certainly like the poor sweet, modest girl, but as a character she mostly bites her tongue and tries to be as good as possible while never, ever saying anything that might make anyone else unhappy, even the jerk-ass playboy who decides he wants to marry her without regard for her feelings on the subject.<br /><br />Naturally, there is another man easily identified as Fanny's One True Love, though while the reader knows who Fanny will marry by the end of the first chapter (even if you've never read Austen before), neither of them realizes it until nearly the last chapter.<br /><br />Very Austenian, but almost generic Austen, if one can say that about such a famous author who only ever wrote six books. To be quite honest, Austen's lovely style and occasionally amusing bits of dialog were all that carried me through this book, particularly some long tedious middle parts. I wanted to love you more, <em>Mansfield Park</em>, but I can only give you 3.5 stars, and half a star is charity.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="2402798688973928675">...more</a>

          </span>

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/7513636-martha?shelf=favorites" class="actionLinkLite">favorites</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer266810599">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15168400869231012044">This is my favourite of Jane Austen's novels. I appreciate the wit of Elizabeth Bennett, and I understand why people might be put off by Fanny Price, but I don't find Fanny at all weak or priggish. Fanny is every bit as strong as Elizabeth Bennett, if in a quieter and more thoughtful way. She shows great insight into the people around her and maintains her integrity in the face of overpowering disapproval from those who hold a lot of power over her.<br /><br />Jane Austen is more than Elizabeth Bennett. Eac</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText15168400869231012044">This is my favourite of Jane Austen's novels. I appreciate the wit of Elizabeth Bennett, and I understand why people might be put off by Fanny Price, but I don't find Fanny at all weak or priggish. Fanny is every bit as strong as Elizabeth Bennett, if in a quieter and more thoughtful way. She shows great insight into the people around her and maintains her integrity in the face of overpowering disapproval from those who hold a lot of power over her.<br /><br />Jane Austen is more than Elizabeth Bennett. Each of her heroines is different, as they should be, and Fanny deserves consideration in her own right, not simply by comparison to Austen's more vivacious heroine.<br /><br />I like Austen best for her impeccable writing and her insight into her protagonists' emotions, and Mansfield Park is full of both. Every time I finish it, I want to start back at the beginning again.</span>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_24799255">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/24799255" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/1246025-jessica" class="left imgcol" title="Jessica"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1254757607p2/1246025.jpg" alt="Jessica" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/24799255?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Jun 18, 2008</a>

      <a href="/user/show/1246025-jessica" class="user" title="Jessica">Jessica</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/45032.Mansfield_Park" title="Paperback 9780141439808" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer24799255">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16415502451366600096">This has become one of my favorite Jane Austen books. I could not get through it as a teenager, but now that I am older, I like it and find myself re-reading it often.<br />The heroine, Fanny Price, is unusual for Austen; unlike the confident, clever heroines like Elizabeth, Emma, and the Dashwood sisters, Fanny has no real talents, except that she is kind, a good listener, helpful, and tries to do the right thing. This is sometimes a nice change, though occasionally as a modern women I sometimes find</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText16415502451366600096">This has become one of my favorite Jane Austen books. I could not get through it as a teenager, but now that I am older, I like it and find myself re-reading it often.<br />The heroine, Fanny Price, is unusual for Austen; unlike the confident, clever heroines like Elizabeth, Emma, and the Dashwood sisters, Fanny has no real talents, except that she is kind, a good listener, helpful, and tries to do the right thing. This is sometimes a nice change, though occasionally as a modern women I sometimes find her lack of assertiveness trying.<br />I also really like the other characters. Well, I don't like them, persay, I just enjoy reading about them. The Bertram sisters are ridiculous in their attempts to one-up the other. Maria especially received my contempt. Her father even offers her the option to break of her engagement her stupid fiance, but she does not take his kind offer. Then she runs off and leaves her stupid husband, when she could have saved herself all that trouble. Mrs. Norris is also very exasperating, but funny because we all know someone who always wants to do everything and receive all the credit. (This, incidently, is the source of Filch's spying cat in the Harry Potter books.)<br />I do end the book feeling sorry for the Crawfords. Henry, who was starting to become a good person because of his love for Fanny, falls even lower. He is one of the most interesting characters in teh book. They deserve, in some respects, better than what Austen gives them.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="16415502451366600096">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">

  <div class="friendReviews elementListBrown">
    <div class="section">
      

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_459373325">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/459373325" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/14591920-sara" class="left imgcol" title="Sara"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1423475285p2/14591920.jpg" alt="Sara" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/459373325?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Aug 24, 2015</a>

      <a href="/user/show/14591920-sara" class="user" title="Sara">Sara</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/10770510-mansfield-park" title="Paperback 9788854119284" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer459373325">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer418404704368463598">Il personaggio di Fanny Price perlopiù non suscita simpatia nel lettore contemporaneo poiché è stato concepito dalla stessa autrice con questo preciso scopo, lo scopo di non piacere. Naturalmente questa sottile intenzione non poteva venire percepita dal lettore georgiano e vittoriano, il quale vedeva di certo nella giovane Price – letteralmente “prezzo”: il prezzo da pagare per essere amata, poiché si sa che la Austen non lasciava niente al caso, neanche i nomi – la perfetta realizzazione della</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText418404704368463598">Il personaggio di Fanny Price perlopiù non suscita simpatia nel lettore contemporaneo poiché è stato concepito dalla stessa autrice con questo preciso scopo, lo scopo di non piacere. Naturalmente questa sottile intenzione non poteva venire percepita dal lettore georgiano e vittoriano, il quale vedeva di certo nella giovane Price – letteralmente “prezzo”: il prezzo da pagare per essere amata, poiché si sa che la Austen non lasciava niente al caso, neanche i nomi – la perfetta realizzazione della fanciulla di buona famiglia, all’interno di un romanzo prettamente didascalico. Il genio di Jane Austen si rivela, a mio avviso, in questo romanzo più che in tutti gli altri proprio per la sua capacità di “confondere le acque”, vestendo il lupo da agnello, nascondendo una critica quantomai feroce e spietata sotto il mantello dell’approvazione.<br />Mentre tutti gli altri romanzi della Austen hanno al centro, appunto, un’eroina – fiera e ribelle (Elizabeth Bennet), forte e coraggiosa (Elinor Dashwood), “sopra le righe” (Catherine Morland ed Emma Woodhouse) o solo libera dai pregiudizi sociali a dispetto dell’ottuso tradizionalismo della propria famiglia (Anne Elliot); in poche parole una creatura catartica, ideata per portare una blanda consolazione a tutte quelle fanciulle che non avevano potuto contrarre un matrimonio d’amore al di sopra (o al di sotto) dei propri mezzi – Mansfield Park rovescia la struttura tradizionale del romanzo austeniano e fa perno attorno all’antieroina, quella che in ogni altro contesto viene relegata al ruolo di contorno di una Charlotte Lucas o di una Jane Fairfax e che rappresenta, nella satira del costume georgiano, la stragrande maggioranza delle situazioni matrimoniali del tempo.<br />Se la Austen avesse perseguito anche in questo romanzo la linea di condotta da lei stessa ideata e sfruttata da Northanger Abbey fino a Ragione e Sentimento, la protagonista di diritto sarebbe stata la spregiudicata e sconveniente Mary Crawford, totalmente inadatta a rivestire il ruolo di consorte del severo Edmund Bertram a causa della sua svincolatezza dall’etichetta e del suo sprezzo per la morale – così come Catherine, Elizabeth ed Elinor sono inadatte a sposare i loro innamorati per via della loro condizione sociale – ma che infine si sarebbe rivelata capace, grazie alla forza d’animo che deriva dall’accettazione di un amore sincero, di attuare un cambiamento, prima di tutto in se stessa, che avrebbe condotto l’eroe a travalicare gli ostacoli al fine di coronare un bel sogno d’amore. Di fatto, il cambiamento in Mary Crawford non avviene mai ed è lei stessa ad allontanare Edmund Bertram – il quale a sua volta sembra incline al mutamento quanto un sasso alla masticazione – spingendolo inconsapevolmente verso Fanny.<br />Al di là della sua apparente semplicità, Mansfield Park è un esperimento complesso e, se si ha l’arguzia di leggere tra le righe, rappresenta anche la denuncia più accorata della Austen alla società del suo tempo, proprio perché finge di accettarne i dettami e le convenzioni anziché ribellarvisi, mostrando cosa accade quando una giovinetta è succube delle “conseguenze dell’educazione” (argomento che tante volte affiora nelle discussioni di Fanny con Edmund). Fanny è infatti descritta più volte come una ragazza vivace, solare, piena di entusiasmi e di interessi come la natura e la poesia, capace di profondo attaccamento e di generosità incondizionata, ma è stata repressa fin da giovanissima, educata a non mettersi in mostra, a essere sempre cortese e pertinente e soprattutto a esprimere giudizi altrui che lei stessa ha finito con l’assumere come propri, tanto da considerare i suoi personali punti di vista – talvolta affioranti in netto e doloroso contrasto con le opinioni dello zio o del cugino – come segnali evidenti di una naturale propensione all’ingratitudine. L’estrema conseguenza di questa formazione imposta e autoimposta è il dirottamento dei desideri di Fanny, la quale si ritrova a desiderare ciò che è stata educata a desiderare, finendo a innamorarsi del Mr. Collins di turno, ovvero Edmund Bertram, anziché del “ribelle” Darcy, qui rappresentato da Henry Crawford, che per amore è “diventato un uomo onesto”: una soluzione, quella del matrimonio di Fanny con Edmund, che compiace la famiglia anziché costringere la coppia a vivere da diseredata o rinnegata, come è accaduto a tutti gli eroi dei precedenti romanzi.<br />Fanny Price è dunque un’eroina scomoda, indigesta, in quanto la lezione fondamentale che ci impartisce non è da rintracciarsi nell’esteriorità della sua condizione, quanto in quello che accade – o, sarebbe più corretto dire, non accade – dentro di lei. Mentre ogni eroina austeniana dei romanzi precedenti e successivi disegna un preciso percorso di progressiva scoperta di sé, acquistando una coscienza personale nell’attimo stesso in cui avviene la scoperta dell’amore – così avviene per Catherine Morland, la quale rivela la sua natura eroica riuscendo a farsi amare al di là della propria condizione; Elizabeth Bennet supera i pregiudizi e le asperità del suo carattere lasciandosi andare all’amore per Darcy; Elinor trova risposta alla domanda “dov’è il tuo cuore?” nello scoppio di gioia con cui infine accoglie Edward; avviene per Emma, che si spoglia dei suoi giochi infantili mentre scopre di vedere in Mr. Knightley qualcosa di più del surrogato di un padre, della cui guida non ha più bisogno; fino ad Anne Elliot, che trova il coraggio di affrancarsi dall’ipocondria nobiliare della propria famiglia liberando il suo amore per il Capitano Wentworth dalla vergogna che le era stato imposto di provare in gioventù – Fanny Price non arriverà mai a compiere questa fondamentale scoperta. La sua storia sembra coronarsi con il consueto lieto fine – sposando l’uomo che ama –, ma è invece tragica e terribile. Perché l’eroina di questa storia non arriverà mai a scoprire chi è davvero Fanny Price, ma vivrà sempre nell’ombra di Mrs. Edmund Bertram, la moglie che il cugino è andato in tanti anni cesellando per se stesso.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="418404704368463598">...more</a>

          </span>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_20685041">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/20685041" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/1078210-amber" class="left imgcol" title="Amber"><img src="http://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/f_50x66-6a03a5c12233c941481992b82eea8d23.png" alt="Amber" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/20685041?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">May 23, 2008</a>

      <a href="/user/show/1078210-amber" class="user" title="Amber">Amber</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/45032.Mansfield_Park" title="Paperback 9780141439808" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer20685041">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3645603522697395863">The first part of this book is a little slow but as you come to the end of the book all of this background information makes the story and characters much more complete in my opinion, and therefore worth the effort. I thoroughly enjoyed this book and was sad to see that so many did not because they were expecting Fanny to be like Elizabeth, or Emma, from Austen's other novels. I, as many, did not first love Fanny but as I came to understand her more I really came to admire her strengths and want</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText3645603522697395863">The first part of this book is a little slow but as you come to the end of the book all of this background information makes the story and characters much more complete in my opinion, and therefore worth the effort. I thoroughly enjoyed this book and was sad to see that so many did not because they were expecting Fanny to be like Elizabeth, or Emma, from Austen's other novels. I, as many, did not first love Fanny but as I came to understand her more I really came to admire her strengths and want to be more like her in many ways. I also don't find her character weak. She stands up for what she believes in and is not so easily swayed as many of the other characters in the book are. She is soft-spoken but I think this just adds to her strength and self control. I would have liked to see her a little more self-confident, but I find her character more believable as it is. I also think that she does become much more confident by the end of the book and I am glad that she doesn't turn out to go to the other extreme of being very vain and self-centered.<br />I also enjoyed that this book wasn't just a romance novel. I think romance is great but I think it is even better when it is coupled with a true and constant love - especially when the book ends in marriage. <br />I haven't really seen many other comments about this but I personally think that this book also carries a moral about being an active parent and practicing what you preach. Maria and Julia are given every advantage, education, status, etc. but they don't truly learn virtue from their parents and this effects what they end up doing with their education and status. Fanny on the other hand was humble when she came to Mansfield Park and really did seem to learn virtue from Edmund (and perhaps she already had a lot of virtue, but we do not know really what she was like before.) Also at the end of the book her uncle takes a much more active role as her guardian. It really inspired me to teach my children to be well mannered and educated, but first and foremost to teach and be an example of morals and virtue. <br /></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="3645603522697395863">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_885526944">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/885526944" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/3642045-sue" class="left imgcol" title="Sue"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1274829293p2/3642045.jpg" alt="Sue" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/885526944?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Apr 09, 2014</a>

      <a href="/user/show/3642045-sue" class="user" title="Sue">Sue</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/8130028-mansfield-park" title="Kindle Edition " class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/3642045-sue?shelf=classics" class="actionLinkLite">classics</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/3642045-sue?shelf=kindle" class="actionLinkLite">kindle</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/3642045-sue?shelf=britain" class="actionLinkLite">britain</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/3642045-sue?shelf=read-2014" class="actionLinkLite">read-2014</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer885526944">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer18217381550088992922">I have not read anything by Jane Austen in years and it really is a delight to discover how much I enjoyed Mansfield Park. This story of Fanny Price, the young girl brought to be raised with her cousins in a life of more purpose and to also be less burden on her own parents, includes all sorts of incidents and episodes one expects in literature of the time and a bit more than I've come to think of as coming from Austen. This is a more grown and mature author we see with more complex plot and cha</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText18217381550088992922">I have not read anything by Jane Austen in years and it really is a delight to discover how much I enjoyed Mansfield Park. This story of Fanny Price, the young girl brought to be raised with her cousins in a life of more purpose and to also be less burden on her own parents, includes all sorts of incidents and episodes one expects in literature of the time and a bit more than I've come to think of as coming from Austen. This is a more grown and mature author we see with more complex plot and characters.<br /><br /><br /><em>This little visitor meanwhile was as unhappy as possible.<br /> Afraid of everybody, ashamed of herself, and longing for<br /> the home she had left, she knew not how to look up, and could<br /> scarcely speak to be heard, or without crying.</em><br /><br /><br />Thus did the child Fanny arrive at Mansfield Park, subject to the glowering gruffness of her uncle, Sir Thomas, the idle wants of Lady Bertram and the not so subtle meanness of her other aunt, Mrs. Norris. Her cousins...the savior there of the two girls and two boys, was the younger son, Edmund who seemed to understand her and her self-deprecating ways.<br /><br />Austen has such a way of "gently" presenting her characters with their warts on full display. Here, Fanny's Bertram cousins, Maria and Julia are caught up in a rivalry.<br /><br /><br /><em>With no material fault of temper, of difference of<br /> opinion, to prevent their being very good friends while<br /> their interests were the same, the sisters, under such a<br /> trial as this, had affection or principle enough to make <br /> them merciful or just, to give them honour or compassion.<br /> Maria felt her triumph, and pursued her purpose, careless<br /> of Julia; and Julia could never see Maria distinguished<br /> by Henry Crawford without trusting that it would create<br /> jealousy, and bring a public disturbance at last.</em><br /><br /><br />And of course the "lovely" Mrs. Norris must be heard from, expounding in all her glory on Fanny's relative worth.<br /><br /><br /><em>"The nonsense and folly of people's stepping out of <br /> their rank and trying to appear above themselves, makes<br /> me think it right to give you a hint, Fanny, now that<br /> you are going into company without any of us; and I do<br /> beseech and entreat you not to be putting yourself<br /> forward, and talking and giving your opinion as if you<br /> were one of your cousins---as if you were dear Mrs.<br /> Rushworth or Julia. That will never do, believe me.<br /> Remember, wherever you are, you must be the lowest and<br /> last....</em><br /><br /><br />Is it any wonder that Fanny is self-effacing?<br /><br />Ah there is so much here. I have not spoken much of the meetings between Fanny and other members of the family and possible suitors. For that you must read the book. If you enjoy Austen, I recommend this book highly. Should you find the beginning a bit slow, look on it as a setting of the stage for all that is to come, a fleshing out of the characters.<br /><br />I particularly loved the final chapter which wonderfully sums up the lives of all. It begins:<br /><br /><br /><em>Let other pens dwell on guilt and misery. I quit such<br /> odious subjects as soon as I can, impatient to restore<br /> everybody, not greatly in fault themselves, to tolerable <br /> comfort, and to have done with all the rest.</em><br /><br /><br />And so, I also have done with all the rest until such time as I read Mansfield Park again when it may well achieve the 5th star. </span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="18217381550088992922">...more</a>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_442123178">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/442123178" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/6610433-midnightfaerie" class="left imgcol" title="midnightfaerie"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1318372863p2/6610433.jpg" alt="midnightfaerie" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/442123178?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Dec 09, 2012</a>

      <a href="/user/show/6610433-midnightfaerie" class="user" title="midnightfaerie">midnightfaerie</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/45032.Mansfield_Park" title="Paperback 9780141439808" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/6610433-midnightfaerie?shelf=classics" class="actionLinkLite">classics</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer442123178">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1433578505210623730"><a rel="nofollow" href="http://classicsdefined.com/austen.html">Click here for Jane Austen Disclaimer </a><br /><br />Mansfield Park by Jane Austen was the last of the Austen tomes I had to read. All that are left are the smaller, more obscure reads. I really enjoyed this one. Fanny was a delightful character who I could really relate to. I loved how she said more by not saying anything a good portion of the time. I didn't agree with the critics who called Fanny "annoying" or "always right". I thought that she was infinitely more complex than that. Just the fact that she co</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText1433578505210623730"><a rel="nofollow" href="http://classicsdefined.com/austen.html">Click here for Jane Austen Disclaimer </a><br /><br />Mansfield Park by Jane Austen was the last of the Austen tomes I had to read. All that are left are the smaller, more obscure reads. I really enjoyed this one. Fanny was a delightful character who I could really relate to. I loved how she said more by not saying anything a good portion of the time. I didn't agree with the critics who called Fanny "annoying" or "always right". I thought that she was infinitely more complex than that. Just the fact that she considered marrying for the "wrong reasons" shows the human side of her and that she was mature enough to see how "marrying for love" like her parents did, could not be the wonderfully happy experience that most fairy tales buy into, without money. We see that she notices more of the dingy aspects of being poor when she comes back to visit her family than she did before and it was more like a statement of fact than a snobbish judgment. It just was what it was. To me, those that are grown, and have seen a little more of life, know that the world is run by money and not love. (I wonder how many criticisms I'll get to that comment?) And although in happy fairy tales such as these, love tends to win out, no one ever seems to mention that, "Oh yeah, by the way, did you notice that she also inherited the house that was as big as an apartment complex?" Makes love a little easier to handle after the "infatuation period" wears off. <br /><br />Fanny was independent, educated, and well written. She had a quiet spark, but a spark nonetheless, and it was a spark that Edmund noticed from the beginning, so we can give him credit as well. I also found the theme of slavery to be interesting. She brings it up just enough to show that it's appalling, yet not enough to bring down the happy tone of the story. What was she trying to say? Was she against it? Was she trying to just give some realism to the current trends of that time period? Did she want to show some different sides to her characters? In any case, I found this novel to be refreshingly different from Austen's other books and after finishing all her novels, most likely, my favorite. Fanny's enchanting persona and how she sees through those around her, to the subtle various themes Austen has underlying the story, makes this (possibly) my favorite Austen novel. It's only other contender is Persuasion, where I see a similar heroine in Anne, soft spoken in voice, yet strong in character. In the end, Austen is talented, no matter which one you read, and I look forward to reading some of her smaller, more intimate works. <br /><br /><br /><a rel="nofollow" href="http://classicsdefined.com/mansfieldpark.html"> ClassicsDefined.com</a><br /><br /><br /></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="1433578505210623730">...more</a>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_1412274058">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1412274058" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/39644765-nocturnalblaze" class="left imgcol" title="NocturnalBlaze"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1436181577p2/39644765.jpg" alt="NocturnalBlaze" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/1412274058?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Nov 15, 2015</a>

      <a href="/user/show/39644765-nocturnalblaze" class="user" title="NocturnalBlaze">NocturnalBlaze</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/18300262-mansfield-park" title="Paperback 9780099589280" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/39644765-nocturnalblaze?shelf=classici" class="actionLinkLite">classici</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/39644765-nocturnalblaze?shelf=letture-in-inglese" class="actionLinkLite">letture-in-inglese</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer1412274058">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6116073931078039957"><em>Letto in inglese / Rilettura</em>.<br />Mansfield Park è un classico godibile, leggero, che offre un bell'affresco dell'età vittoriana e che riesce ad esemplificare, soprattutto grazie allo stile ironico ed intelligente di Jane Austen, tutte le contraddizioni, i pregi ed i difetti che hanno caratterizzato quel periodo storico in Inghilterra. <br />Fanny, la protagonista del romanzo, seguita dalla tarda infanzia fino alla prima giovinezza, è una ragazza con cui è allo stesso tempo facile e difficile sentirsi conn</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText6116073931078039957"><em>Letto in inglese / Rilettura</em>.<br />Mansfield Park è un classico godibile, leggero, che offre un bell'affresco dell'età vittoriana e che riesce ad esemplificare, soprattutto grazie allo stile ironico ed intelligente di Jane Austen, tutte le contraddizioni, i pregi ed i difetti che hanno caratterizzato quel periodo storico in Inghilterra. <br />Fanny, la protagonista del romanzo, seguita dalla tarda infanzia fino alla prima giovinezza, è una ragazza con cui è allo stesso tempo facile e difficile sentirsi connessi: da una parte, la sua estrema timidezza ed il suo costante imbarazzo fanno tenerezza, dall'altra la sua pudicizia ed il generale il suo approcciarsi alla realtà ed agli altri personaggi può sembrare quasi incomprensibile ad un lettore moderno. Eppure è comunque interessante seguirla nella sua crescita, forse proprio perché rappresenta una realtà che ormai non esiste più, dei valori che hanno assunto un importanza minore nel nostro tempo.<br />E' questo un romanzo che si realizza sulle relazioni, sugli intrecci fra personaggi, sulla descrizione della vita di una famiglia della piccola nobiltà della campagna inglese che si scontra con la realtà cittadina, licenziosa e con un sistema valoriale completamente diverso, che si realizza sotto forma di vari personaggi che andranno ad intaccare l'apparente equilibrio della tenuta di Mansfield, sconvolgendo i suoi abitanti. E' dunque fondamentale la dimensione del rapporto amoroso, del matrimonio e della conseguente acquisizione o perdita di prestigio sociale, il discorso legato all'avanzamento di classe, al denaro, alla rispettabilità, tutte tematiche classiche del periodo, reinterpretate dalla Austen attraverso una ironica lente di denuncia ed un tocco frizzante e lieve nello stile. <br />Un'opera a mio parere godibile anche da un lettore moderno, proprio in virtù dello stile scorrevole, semplice e coinvolgente dell'autrice, che offre un buon quadro dell'epoca, mostrandone la superficialità attraverso la parabola di crescita di un personaggio buono, innocente, una giovane donna portatrice di valori saldi, in contrasto con la mentalità comune volta solo al conseguimento dell'interesse personale. <br />E' un romanzo di formazione che intrattiene, diverte nel suo rappresentare una società distante, fatta di facezie, di personaggi pieni di pregi e difetti, di grandi ville, di occupazioni dilettevoli e poco significative, godibilissimo pur essendo un classico, proprio perché riesce a scorrere lieve grazie ai numerosi intrecci, scandali, avvenimenti inaspettati all'interno di un contesto solo apparentemente immobile e faceto. </span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="6116073931078039957">...more</a>

Through a series of vignettes, Elizabeth Gaskell portrays a community governed by old-fashioned habits and dominated by friendships between women. Her wry account of rural life is undercut, however, by tragedy in its depiction of such troubling events as Matty\'s bankruptcy, the violent death of Captain Brown or the unwitting cruelty of Peter Jenkyns. Written with acute observation, Cranford is by turns affectionate, moving and darkly satirical.\n\nIn her introduction, Patricia Ingham discusses Cranford in relation to Gaskell\'s own past and as a work of irony in the manner of Jane Austen. She also considers the implications of the novel in terms of class and empire. This edition also includes further reading, notes, and an appendix on the significance of \'Fashion at Cranford\'.&lt;\/span&gt;\n  &lt;a data-text-id=\"1592795188676901166\" href=\"#\" One is the shy Caroline Helstone, who is trapped in the oppressive atmosphere of a Yorkshire rectory and whose bare life symbolizes the plight of single women in the nineteenth century. The other is the vivacious Shirley Keeldar, who inherits a local estate and whose wealth liberates her from convention.A work that combines social commentary with the more private preoccupations of Jane Eyre, Shirley demonstrates the full range of Brontë\'s literary talent. &amp;quot;Shirley is a revolutionary novel,&amp;quot; wrote Brontë biographer Lyndall Gordon. &amp;quot;Shirley follows Jane Eyre as a new exemplar but so much a forerunner of the feminist of the later twentieth century that it is hard to believe in her actual existence in 1811-12. She is a theoretic possibility: what a woman might be if she combined independence and means of her own with intellect. Charlotte Brontë imagined a new form of power, equal to that of men, in a confident young woman [whose] extraordinary freedom has accustomed her to think for herself....Shirley [is] Brontë\'s most     <div class=" clearFloats bigBox" id="aboutAuthor"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/1265.Jane_Austen">About Jane Austen</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
      <a href="/author/show/1265.Jane_Austen"><img alt="1265" src="http://d.gr-assets.com/authors/1380085320p2/1265.jpg" class="right" /></a>
    <div class="readable">
      
<span id="freeTextContainer10941234150882306260">Jane Austen (16 December 1775 – 18 July 1817) was an English novelist whose works of romantic fiction, set among the landed gentry, earned her a place as one of the most widely read writers in English literature, her realism and biting social commentary cementing her historical importance among scholars and critics.<br /><br />Austen lived her entire life as part of a close-knit family located on the lower fr</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText10941234150882306260">Jane Austen (16 December 1775 – 18 July 1817) was an English novelist whose works of romantic fiction, set among the landed gentry, earned her a place as one of the most widely read writers in English literature, her realism and biting social commentary cementing her historical importance among scholars and critics.<br /><br />Austen lived her entire life as part of a close-knit family located on the lower fringes of the English landed gentry. She was educated primarily by her father and older brothers as well as through her own reading. The steadfast support of her family was critical to her development as a professional writer. Her artistic apprenticeship lasted from her teenage years until she was about 35 years old. During this period, she experimented with various literary forms, including the epistolary novel which she tried then abandoned, and wrote and extensively revised three major novels and began a fourth. From 1811 until 1816, with the release of Sense and Sensibility (1811), Pride and Prejudice (1813), Mansfield Park (1814) and Emma (1815), she achieved success as a published writer. She wrote two additional novels, Northanger Abbey and Persuasion, both published posthumously in 1818, and began a third, which was eventually titled Sanditon, but died before completing it.<br /><br />Austen's works critique the novels of sensibility of the second half of the 18th century and are part of the transition to 19th-century realism. Her plots, though fundamentally comic, highlight the dependence of women on marriage to secure social standing and economic security. Her work brought her little personal fame and only a few positive reviews during her lifetime, but the publication in 1869 of her nephew's A Memoir of Jane Austen introduced her to a wider public, and by the 1940s she had become widely accepted in academia as a great English writer. The second half of the 20th century saw a proliferation of Austen scholarship and the emergence of a Janeite fan culture.<br /></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="10941234150882306260">...more</a>

    </div>
